I‚Äôve heard just about everyone comment on the cutthroat Harfoots in this episode, regarding the migration thingy, and I had an almost immediately different take on it. Hear me out on this: It seems to me that they have ZERO fighting prowess. Like, they DO NOT fight to survive. So, why does this make their migrations seem so heartless to us, but is really a desperate and tragic affair for them?  Look at they lengths they go to camouflage themselves. They do it as part of everyday life, and the time it must take to prepare and maintain all that means they probably don‚Äôt invest any time in other means of dealing with threats, such as honing martial skills.   They‚Äôre nomadic and completely wild. They have no cities for housing, no walls for defence. They‚Äôre always and at all times open to whatever predator could just wander into their camp and start snacking. That must have a profound effect on their psychology, and their instinct when faced with danger is either to hide or flee.   I think considering these traits, and learned from hard experience, they know that while they‚Äôre undergoing the migration, they‚Äôre more vulnerable than ever, and though they truly must loathe it, for the society as a whole to survive, they can‚Äôt afford to stop moving during that migration for even a moment. What if they turn back to help a straggler, and wind up losing so many more of their group‚Äôs precious few individuals? Any attempt to fight off attackers could easily lead to more deaths, because these people DO NOT FIGHT. They run, and that‚Äôs how they‚Äôve endured.   I know I‚Äôm making a LOT of assumptions based on stupidly little information, but the Harfoot custom regarding those who fall behind on their migrations strikes me as, from their primitive point of view, both sadly necessary, and desperately sad.

"The sea is always right"  Super basic? As apparent as it seems it's odd to dismiss as something not worthy of inclusion. It speaks to the wisdom of their military culture, one bred on the experience of countless deaths at sea. It's a nice contrast to other parts of their society where some carry themselves with hubris, and others with hurt pride. This detail is quite a significant piece of the puzzle fleshing out numenor as a complete society in the shows lore. We get to see that some still remember that they are nothing but specks of dust in the face of the valar, so much so that they struggle to survive the nature created.  Seems super basic to miss that. First, I like your analysis and breakdown of the episode. It was very forthright and well researched.  Second, I will admit that I was not a big fan of the series going in especially after finding out a lot of the changes being made (no I'm not an absolute purist, just appreciate the work Tolkien di to flesh out His world). Though I am still watching the show to see if they will eventually correct some of the bigger mistakes they have made like the almost subtle way the show writers are seemingly trying to get around their I.P. restrictions and change the history of Middle-Earth (i.e. not even mentioning that the war against Morgoth was in Beleriand not Middle-Earth proper or that the men of the "Southlands" were loyal to Morgoth). Also, the time frame they are working around doesn't fit with what Tolkien wrote as the overall timeline of the Second Age. For example, in the show the idea for the Elven rings of power is just emerging, but in this episode, we're introduced to Elendil who, according to Tolkien's timeline if I remember correctly, wouldn't have even been born yet as that event took place mid Second Age and there was no way that Elendil was over 1000 years old during the War of the Last Alliance that we see in the first LOTR movie. As to the men of the "southlands" thing my issue with that is that Morgoth didn't have a presence that far into Middle-Earth, nor did he even know about the race of Man until about the time of the third war in Beleriand during the War of the Jewels. And when he did make alliance with Men it was the Easterlings that came to ally/worship him. This plus some other things (No, not the racial diversity of the cast before anyone says anything. I couldn't give two ffffs about that issue) are kinda turning me off the show right now. Again, I'm still watching in hope that it gets better, but I'm not holding my breath. Amazon should have really learned their lesson with the Whell of Time adaptation that they're putting out(?). In defense of the Harfoots I think if you understand the cultures of any migratory and nomadic peoples in early tool age cultures it‚Äôs not unreasonable to believe and see that they wouldn‚Äôt go out of their way and endanger the whole tribe if only a few were to be lost. The reasons for the losses also were very much out of their control. Landslides, wolves, etc.   I actually really enjoy the Harfoots storyline. Just because Hobbits are only mentioned in Tolkiens lore in the third age it doesn‚Äôt make sense that they‚Äôd just suddenly appear. This version of them living in total seclusion and secrecy, hiding from men, elves and orcs alike, gives a general reason why elves and humans never mention them in their histories until much later. Hobbits have to ‚Äúdescend‚Äù from somewhere. This is a reasonable take in my belief. I feel like everyone is really ripping on the Harfoots existing with the very poor excuse that Tolkien doesn‚Äôt bring up Hobbits existence until the third age. As if that‚Äôs explanation enough for their origin. Middle earth is full of mysteries and the hobbits origins are one of them. Since they are not a direct creation of El Iluvatar (by themselves anyway, since they‚Äôre likely an offshoot of humans). That's why he's showing the stars - he's saying that's me, I'm one of them.  It was a reply in kind after Nori told him her name. He isn't good or evil, he is star-stuff. The crater formed by the impact looked like the One Ring encircling him in it's centre. When he stood up the fire in the crater went out, and when he fell back down the fire ignited again. At one point you could hear whispers like the whispering of the One Ring. This will be the reason the One Ring is all powerful - not just because Sauron puts much of himself into it, but also because it is forged from a living star. Another prediction I'll make now is that there will be significant time skips in between seasons, because the only conclusion to the whole series that makes sense is the defeat of Sauron by the Last Alliance and Isildur's refusal to destroy the Ring. I know we are all a bit apprehensive with the harfoots in this series, waiting to see if they're going to influence on something they are no supposed to. But I have to confess that I found this episode a fantastic portrait of a race. Since they are not mentioned in the second age, the show has a bit of wiggle room to tell the stories of this proto-hobbits. And I was taken away by Sadoc's speech, and their culture of survival, which provides an interesting point of comparison with the hobbits we know, creatures that much love their lands in The Shire, love confort and good food, are weary of outsiders and mostly don't get involved in their affairs. Hobbits' hoarding nature, Bilbo's care for his house and his family possessions in his early years, the "no adventures here" policy by the many nosy hobbits in The Shire, could be interpreted, in my opinion - which is not in any way of a scholar, but just an admirer and layman to tolkien issues -  as a consequence to the sufferings and hard times lived by these harfoots in early days in their nomadic roots. I'm loving the moderate discussions here, and would like to know the opinions of more knowlegeable people here about this. My insanely long review of RoP with SPOILERS.  Let me start by saying: I thought it was going to be great. I love Tolkien and Amazon. I genuinely thought Amazon was the only one who had the right studio to pull it off, so I was in camp of this is going to be amazing. HBO would have sexed it up, STARZ would have made it a melodrama, and Netflix would have just randomly canceled after a successful season for no reason. Then the trailers dropped and the script seemed off, but I chalked it up to just bad trailer editing. However, after seeing three episodes, this is worse than Wheel of Time!  I re-watched with 7 friends. 3 hardcore Tolkien geeks, 1 Tolkien lover, 2 Tolkien enjoyers, and 1 general fantasy lover who knows nothing of Lord of the Rings.  The 3 Tolkien nerds: At best 10% accurate to the lore, changing fundamental things in the text, slow and boring with horrible writing.  The 1 Tolkien lover (a female who's favorite character is Galadriel, both in the books and movies): How could they destroy Galadriel like this? She is one of the most powerful female characters in all of Tolkien and they reduced her to a basic warrior who's personality is muddled with toxic masculinity. 3 hours in and literally nothing has happened. Why is everyone talking so pretentious, yet the words they say do not match the scene?  2 Tolkien enjoyers: The Hobbits are annoying like child actors and everything that was "cute" about them in the first episode is thrown out the window in episode 3 when they turn them into vicious, sycophants who leave their loved ones for dead while chanting, "no one goes off alone." Why is everyone staring at each other for such long periods of time? On the 4k 75" TV, everything looks clearly CGI. Even PJ's LOTR effects were better than this.  1 general fantasy lover: *fell asleep* Sooooo boring. More boring than the movies. What is even going on? Why are all the fight scenes like anime? --------------------------------------------------------------------------- Show Quality (Not Relating to Lore):  Writing - This is the chief weakness of the show. First, there is a large team of writers and it is extremely obvious. The scenes are clearly written by different people with inconsistent pacing and usage of language. It is actually jarring sometimes the sort of emotional whiplash that occurs between serious and slap-stick scenes. Second, the plot and all that happens seems to be driven by a series of "good ideas" strung together by filler. For example, it is like someone said, "Wouldn't it be cool if Arondir was captured and he tried to escape by doing a cool flip to cut down the sun barriers protecting the orcs?" And then from there, the team wrote a convoluted plot as to why orcs will dig a trench with cloth covering for hundreds of miles rather than tunnels or just digging at night. Thirdly, and worst is the dialog. The showrunners came out and said that when crafting the speech of the characters, they looked at Tolkien's written work on those characters and tried to have the writers match the poetic meter that those characters talk in. This sounds amazing in theory. However, what we end up with are EXTREMELY long and nonsensical lines that sound deep because the average person can't understand the full meaning without a second or third re-watch/listen. For example, in the very first episode we get Finrod telling Galadriel that stones sink in water because they "look down" while ships "look up". This is the writers' attempt to create a Tolkienien personification of objects to teach a lesson. However, personifications still require some sort of connection to reality to have them be meaningful. Another example is the significant amount of non sequitur responses people make to questions or statements made. In one scene 'Elrond asks 'Galadriel (paraphrased into modern text to visualize its absurdity), "How are you Galadriel? Tell me about your journey." 'Galadriel's response, "Do not give me fake compliments!" Her response is non sequitur as 'Elrond never gave her a compliment. He simply asked how she was doing. If you watch carefully, these sort of non sequitur responses happen in ALMOST EVERY SCENE. I am not exaggerating, but they are easy to miss if you are not paying attention.  Acting: I hate judging acting. So many times, people criticize actors saying they are bad, but who knows how many good takes there were and the director just chose the worst. I do not want to judge the actors harshly because the writing is so poor. What I can say is Disa and Durin are the two most likeable characters. This could be partially due to how they are written, but these two actors actually have chemistry. Their scene together in episode 2 is the ONLY bright spot in the first three episodes. The actor who plays Elrond said he cares about the lore and I can feel the tension as he tries to bring more to his character than was written for this show.  Special Effects: Clearly this is where most of the money went. The sweeping shots of New Zealand are beautiful and the sets are generally well done, but there is a grandness missing due to the heavy reliance on CGI and green screens. On our 75" 4k TV, the show looks very video game-y which is a bit distracting.  Costumes and props: some high highs and some low lows. Especially on the big screen, some props look REALLY fake and plastic-y. The armor is definitely the worst in the show, clearly bending rubber or printed spandex instead of higher quality realistic looking armor. Prosthetics are obvious with bald-cap lines showing and the trims are obviously machine made too modern to the time period.   --------------------------------------------------------------------------- Lore:  I understand that there is not 1000 pages of text to go off of like Lord of the Rings. Tolkien practically wrote a history book, an OUTLINE of the major events with little detail around the 2nd Age. However, what we were expecting was for the writers of the show to follow the MINIMAL outline out there and fill in the gaps. However, thus far, they have fundamentally changed written lore in almost every major event and character.  The main problem is they gave Galadriel, Tolkien's most graceful and powerful sorceress the arc and personality of Feanor, Tolkien's biggest Elven d-bag. Even if we take Tolkien's latest draft notes on Galadriel that she defended the Teleri in the Kingslaying, and take that literally with sword and shield, that still does not make her a mean she is a horrific leader. She has always inspired those around her. The only reason she doesn't return to Valinor is because of pride not revenge. It is a complete change to written lore, not just filling in the blanks with her.  Let's do a quick word search in the Silmarillion: Revenge: 3 times - twice about Melkor taking revenge and once about Sauron's evil men taking revenge.  Vengeance - 13 times - seven are ALL about Feanor's oath, even the one time Galadriel is telling Melian about it as Feanor's vengeance not her own. One reference is about Morgoth's vengeance. One is the dwarves' vengeance and the last four are about Beren, Children of Hurin, and other mannish vengeance.  Galadriel is NEVER once EVER associated with vengeance. Further, in the latest drafts of Tolkien's writing, Galadriel DEFENDED the Teleri against Feanor making her further from that.  Additionally, it states, "No oaths she swore, but the words of F√´anor concerning Middle-earth had kindled in her heart, for she yearned to see the wide unguarded lands and to rule there a realm at her own will."  This EXPLICITLY states she swore no oaths. Prime-Galadriel ('Galadriel) is just different. If you like it, fine. Just do not claim she is aligned to Tolkien.

Tolkien simply gave us his beloved languages, that developed in a diverse, culturally rich world that is unlike ours. One of his making, describing the nature he loved and one in which magic is steadily fading.  Then he wrote a story into this world he developed until his death.  No politics, no hidden messages, no allegories, it's timeless.  He did not even expect others to share his interest and was proven wrong when his books were published.  It shows courage and cowardliness, honesty and deceit, prejudice and open-mindedness, life and death, mistrust of strangers finally leading to cooperation, hope yet no certainty, etc.   All universal concepts in a setting that isn't our modern world or even a 1:1 representation of our past. It simply is HIS and it should not be disrespected by people claiming to know better/ to be able to "fix" Tolkien's Middle Earth so that it can be like ours.   They don't even care about his story, only how they can use a semblance of it for their agenda, their messages crammed into his independent world to highlight and to influence opinions about the real world.   It's not wanted or needed!  And when we say exactly that, they use stigmas to belittle our opinions and paint us as small-minded bigots. They ridicule that we care about a literary masterpiece to such an extent, (tell us to get a life and basically to stop caring about it at all, because it isn't real, it doesn't matter what they do to it)   Instead of listening to our criticism about how they misrepresent and change things that are not necessary, they say we aren't welcome in the fandom anymore.  They are drawing lines to defend a story that isn't Tolkien and claim it's the new "fixed" Tolkien that's open to all....and don't see the irony of that statement, don't mention they mean open to everyone that agrees with their methods.  Someone who does not like it, for whatever reason, is apparently against them and therefore on some level X-phobic/-ist, etc   His story was already translated to dozens of languages, well received all over the world, simply appreciated for what it is.  Now they are trying to claim it wasn't so and that they are the only ones able to do make that happen.  That some people could not truly enjoy it before because of the creator of the entire world and story.  It's sad...and I will never complain about the Tolkien estate not selling rights to his works ever again

My dad was a VFX art designer on the show, and he got to walk around some of the sets. You'd be surprised how much stuff was built. He said the whole Armenelos port was built as well as many streets, the blacksmith was all working and crammed with details. The ship that Elendil was on at the start of the episode was also fully built out. The statue in the scene with Galadriel and Halbrand was also built over many weeks for that one 3-4 second shot. It wasn't just the sets, he said the costumes they made for each character were unbelievably detailed. It was quite nice to hear how much of the sets were actually built and weren't just CGI. Just thought that might be fun to share as I keep hearing how it looks 'fake' or 'like cosplay'. The amount of work put into this show is staggering

Why should we care about Elrond or Gil-galed if we don‚Äôt see them before hand. Adar is an Elf who has been corrupted and leads the Orcs of the Southlands.  Leaks from the show‚Äôs set detail how Adar could be seen alongside a mysterious and intimidating hooded character who could be the real Sauron, with Adar being one of his lieutenants.  In the Elvish language of Sindarin, ‚Äòadar‚Äô translates to ‚Äòfather‚Äô so the Orcs referring to him by that name could mean that he is their creator.  During the film adaptation of The Fellowship of the Ring, we learn from Saruman that the Orcs were once Elves who were so badly tortured and mutilated that they eventually became the wretched creatures we see now.  Adar could be working for Sauron and helping to build up his forces in the Southlands in preparation to reveal his presence fully or to distract from his secretive plans elsewhere in Middle-earth, such as arranging for the creation of the Rings of Power.  However, an alternate theory suggested that Adar could indeed be Sauron and the hooded figure is actually one of the Nazgul (Ringwraiths) who served Sauron after being corrupted by the Rings of Power ‚Äì although, none of the Rings of Power have been created in the series‚Äô timeline just yet, which makes this theory unlikely for the time being. On a linguistic side note, Tolkien knew the word "elf" is derived from the Germanic word *ALBI which is from the Latin word Albis for "river bed". Old Norse river name Elfr, Swedish √§lv "river", Norwegian elv "river", Old English river name elf, and Middle Low German elve "river-bed", which also seems to have had the colloquial name for the "river people", like it's twin in Old English "Wik" (which meant "creek"), and later became the name for the people who lived and spread up the river, the "Wikings"...better known today as the Vikings.  So it's nice to see this version of Tolkien's world depict this this strong connection to his water people....his Mermen*. ;)  citation: Orel, Vladimir. A Handbook of Germanic Etymology. Leiden, Netherlands: Brill, 2003: 13  * similarly MER is a proto-germanic word for Sea https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/mer-

Personally, I was quite sad at the level of gore, particularly with the warg. It doesn't really add anything to the drama other than mere shock, and neither does it invite you to use any imagination, which stories are meant to do - it only shocks you with how unimaginative it is. It's one of the reasons I really love theatre these days, you have to use your sympathetic imagination in order to truly appreciate it. Film and TV can stray into the rut of reality-porn that renders the viewer as someone incapable of thinking for themselves, which renders them incapable of genuine sympathy, which is something you can only do if you do it freely. So, such explicit depictions that are irrelevant to the narrative point actually lessen the emotional weight of the moment. That's my two cents at least.

This video helped me see a lot of things I missed in this episode.  However, I disagree with the comment that Galadriel's assertion that her meeting with the "new Aragorn" was no accident was just wishful thinking for Galadriel.  I think the writer's were honoring a very dominant theme in Tolkien's writing that in the midst of all that was happening on the ground in Middle Earth, there was an unseen hand moving behind the scenes working on behalf of the characters who were fighting for good. You see it in the dialog where Gandalf tells Frodo that Bilbo's merciful choice not to kill Gollum when he had the chance could be a decision with important implications later, and that Gollum might have a part to play that shouldn't be interfered with.  You also see it in the unfolding circmstances and the timing of things that could be interpreted as merely for dramatic effect but were more importantly evidence of a guiding hand that worked on behalf of the characters.  In episode 3, I think Galadriel was giving voice to that.

So far (not this episode so much) I find the Harfoots interesting for several reasons. They are a relatively small groups that don't settle down in any one place permanently or have farms/fields, much like our ancient hunter-gatherer ancestors. Those who research into climate change, will know what I am poking at now. Many civilisations and groups/races of people do not live in balance with nature (mother nature) and pay the price for it...  But they (Harfoots) keep to themselves, and this is where we could find some interesting stories with some of the Harfoots getting involved with the Blue Wizards, men, Dwarves and Elves, and even teaching them somethings about living in peace and harmony with the natural world. Some of this learnings and quiet wisdom, pass down to the later wizards and hobbits, even men.

Did some deep digging on the cast, and the face shape of adar at the end of the episode really matched Joseph Mawle, whose character is listen as Oren. This led me to an article in February that gave me this: Oren is supposedly the codename for a character named Arden, a villain in the series; Arden is quite possibly the series‚Äô main villain, supposedly an Elf who directly serves Sauron. In the casting call, the character was described as follows: ‚ÄúA villain who can also evoke a deep sense of pathos and wounded / fallen nobility. Must possess a certain degree of physicality. Should seem middle-aged, though must also project a sense of timelessness.‚Äù  That same article updates Arden to Adar. This makes a lot of sense considering this is a 50 episode series arc. Thank you for not being a hater. I am a deep ME lore-head, and there is a lot i instinctively don't like about this adaptation (though that isn't the right word), so i enjoy hearing your comments and feelings - this week you reminded me about something I had forgotten about the PJ movies: For all their craft and commitment they still seemed like Fairy Stories, and this is a weakness in the source material. Whilst he was a great crafter of Myth, JRRT's world is pretty fake, a painted set in front of which stories unfold. The Shire has some depth, but none of his other settings, magical and epic as they are, have a feeling of lived-in reality. Personally I think his characters are mostly pretty hollow too - archetypes at best. I was reminded of this when you were comparing Armeneloth with Minas Tirith. This series has many challenges beyond working from scant and fragmented sources whilst navigating rights issues - It has taken on the daunting task of making this world believable and attractive to a casual contemporary viewer, whereas all PJ needed to do was retell the story. I remember how excited I was to see places like Minas Tirith, but it never looked or felt like a real city where people lived real lives.  So I have to give credit to the series for trying to fill out this world and bring it to life. There are many problems and much to cringe at, but I will continue to support it and look for things to admire.

The biggest glaring issue here is the deviation from so many well established events, and the avoidance of the true established timeline, that we know based on tolkiens writings, actually happened in certain order and over a long period of time. This show seems to be condensing and going with it.  Examples: Elendil and Isildur (hopefully anarion) are alive and well when there is no mention of any rings of power being forged (over 1000 years they were created and distributed before elendil was born),   Celebrimbor is still alive, and not even a mention of imladris/Rivendell being around (given Elrond apparently still lives in Lindon), all the while no rings are around and no war of Sauron and elves.    This could all change, but I‚Äôm hoping this is a rights issue rather than attempt to depict Tolkien lore that is holding them back. As it stands now, this seems to be mostly fan fiction with Tolkien names more than anything.  I know how well read you are in Tolkien, which is why I love your channel and listen often. Are you really able to look past/over all these major events? As I said above, there could be hope for change, but even the key character, Galadriel, is far different from the actual books and writings (appendices and notes included), than what we are seeing here. The Amazon series makes crazy jumps, sometimes of thousands of years in the 2nd age. After Galadriel in the first few centuries hunts Sauron we're then taken with no apparent time gap, to the time Celibrimbor is planning his big project which in the actual storyline is around 1000 years after the first episode. And Sauron should have first appeared in Celebrimbors time of his big project and Sauron forged the One Ring in 1600 of the 2nd age. The elves figure him out and the Numenoreans sail over the kick Sauron's ass.   It is the year 3255 when Ar-Pharazon becomes King and sails to ME and captures Sauron in 3261 and brings him to Numenor and becomes corrupted. The series therefore has very little relation to the actual Tolkien storylines. They're trying to mix in everything from the 2nd age into one timeframe.

I REALLY wanted to like this series. Funnily enough, it's not the story itself that bothers me, which I feared, but the look and feel of the series. And what I thought was maybe just teething problems in the first two episodes became blatantly obvious in this one - I liked it the least so far. On a positive note, I like Elendil and N√∫menor looks great for the most part. I also like Halbrand and find his story arc and the red herrings they're probably throwing us quite intriguing. But I have massive issues with the storytelling:  1. The acting is still rather wooden and choppy. Everybody is speaking like on a theatre stage, including the Harfoots. Over the top intonation, theatrical pauses, always a bit too loud. I was okay with this sort of diction with the elves, but everybody using this pseudo-antiquated speech is very tiring.  2. The costumes are TERRIBLE. So far, not a single one (maybe with the exception of the dwarves) looks good, let alone believable. The material looks cheap, the colours are odd (what's with the orange and blue colour-coded N√∫menorians? And why is everyone into shapeless tunics these days? Where's the layering? The embroidery? The elegance you would expect from both Elves and N√∫menorians?) and they don't function in the world we're seeing. Why does Halbrand look like he's escaped the sea monster 100 times over and Galadriel's nightgown has - at most - a stylish ombre-looking rim that MIGHT indicate dirt. I get it, elves are magic and aetherial, but surely that wouldn't extend to their clothes after surviving a mile-long swim, a storm, a sea-monster, a near-drowning...?  3. The same goes for a number of set designs. Especially the little Harfoot "village" often looks like stage props plonked down randomly and they scream "look how otherworldly this prop is", rather than creating actual set pieces that are usable. I'm thinking especially of the scene where Nori's cart falls behind. You can see that the other wagons all have this reed material on top to camouflage them - a really pretty shot when they are trotting through the same reed. But minutes later, they are in a completely different surrounding with a grassy (?) plane - so their camouflage would no longer work. But surely they don't redecorate their wagons every 20 minutes or so? This is what I loved about LotR so much - every prop, every costume served a purpose and it worked in any given circumstance. Here, we get pretty looks, but no practical ones.  4. Which brings me to storytelling. They still don't grasp the concept of "show, don't tell". Again, heavy exposition dumping in this episode and no real plot or character development. Instead we get these long speeches like at the Harfoot's or the rather odd conversation between Elendil and his daughter. In addition, just like the sets, the scenes don't always follow logic. Take the scene when Halbrand hands Galadriel her dagger. I like the idea as such - but WHY essentially in front of a hall full of hostile (to them) people? And where are the guards we saw a second earlier; while they just take 2 minutes to exchange AND posess a deadly weapon? It serves the plot, but it's not logical.  5. And yes, finally I have to admit that I tried to like Galadriel. I tried to convince myself that she's the Galadriel that just left Valinor, eager to rule, slightly hot-headed... But it's so in-your-face. She kills the snow troll while her male buddies are just standing around. She figures out the Mordor map thing while the male librarians, despite the map being RIGHT THERE, etc. couldn't... And all that while being very rude to everyone else. Don't get me wrong. She doesn't need to have Cate Blanchett's aura and refined speech yet. But she is the daughter of a royal house, an elf and a few thousand years old - she should know how to behave in court and be able to at least PRETEND to be diplomatic and not behave like a spoilt teenager. Most humans learn that in their late teens, I'm pretty sure Galadriel would've, too. I think she has an interesting story to tell, I just wish they would give her more than one dimension and maybe a bit of struggle every once in a while.  So yeah. I was really disappointed by this episode. I'm of course looking forward to the next ones, but boy, this was a bit of a letdown for me :(

So I think seeing Elendil in this episode really drove home how to think of this series: an alternate universe of Middle Earth.  If it were me making the story and I wanted end of second age characters in a story that is supposed to take place around 1,500-1,600SA, I would have just made the story last thousands of years, not crunched disparate story lines together.  I wouldn‚Äôt have made SA Galadriel act  and do deeds like FA and even pre-FA Galadriel.  I think I would have kept to the Tolkien established timeline but fleshed out non-canonical characters into the canonical timeline to tell the story that I wanted, √† la most of the video game adaptations.  Accepting that this isn‚Äôt the choice that the writers/directors made, I can enjoy the show for what it is.  To me, the stronger elements are the well written and acted non-canonical characters, Arondir (Woodwatch in Sindaran) being the best so far, the visuals, and the faithful usage of the various elven languages.  I find myself pausing after hearing elvish then consulting my handy ‚ÄúLanguages of Tolkien‚Äôs Middle Earth‚Äù by Ruth S. Noel to verify what is said and every time I‚Äôve found the elvish to be accurate and make sense in the context.
